	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Lee_Bouvier&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacqueline Lee Bouvier">Jacqueline Lee Bouvier</a>)</span></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Jacqueline Bouvier" redirects here. For the fictional animated character, see <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Bouvier_(Simpsons)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacqueline Bouvier (Simpsons)">Jacqueline Bouvier (Simpsons)</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Jackie O" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Jackie_O_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Jackie O (disambiguation)">Jackie O (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Public figure, First Lady to 35th U.S. President John F. Kennedy</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p><b>Jacqueline Lee Kennedy Onassis</b> (<a href="/wiki/Maiden_and_married_names" title="Maiden and married names"><i>née</i></a> <b>Bouvier</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/uː/: &#39;oo&#39; in &#39;goose&#39;">uː</span><span title="&#39;v&#39; in &#39;vie&#39;">v</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span><span title="/eɪ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;face&#39;">eɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span>; July 28, 1929 – May 19, 1994) was <a href="/wiki/First_Lady_of_the_United_States" title="First Lady of the United States">First Lady of the United States</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Presidency of John F. Kennedy">presidency of</a> <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> and was regarded then and afterward as an international icon of style and culture.
</p><p>Bouvier was born in 1929 in <a href="/wiki/Southampton,_New_York" title="Southampton, New York">Southampton, New York</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a> stockbroker <a href="/wiki/John_Vernou_Bouvier_III" title="John Vernou Bouvier III">John Vernou Bouvier III</a> and his wife, <a href="/wiki/Janet_Lee_Bouvier" title="Janet Lee Bouvier">Janet Lee Bouvier</a>. In 1951, she graduated with a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Arts" title="Bachelor of Arts">Bachelor of Arts</a> in <a href="/wiki/French_literature" title="French literature">French literature</a> from <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_University" title="George Washington University">George Washington University</a> and went on to work for the <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Times-Herald" title="Washington Times-Herald">Washington Times-Herald</a></i> as an inquiring photographer.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1952, Bouvier met then-<a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">Congressman</a> Jack Kennedy at a dinner party in Washington. Kennedy was elected to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a> that same year, and the couple married on September 12, 1953, in <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Rhode_Island" title="Newport, Rhode Island">Newport, Rhode Island</a>. They had four children, two of whom <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_curse" title="Kennedy curse">died in infancy</a>. Following her husband's <a href="/wiki/Election_of_John_F._Kennedy_as_President_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Election of John F. Kennedy as President of the United States">election to the presidency</a> in <a href="/wiki/1960_United_States_presidential_election" title="1960 United States presidential election">1960</a>, Jacqueline was known for her highly publicized restoration of the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> and emphasis on arts and culture, as well as for her style, elegance, and grace.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> At age 31, she was the third youngest <a href="/wiki/First_Lady_of_the_United_States" title="First Lady of the United States">First Lady</a> when her husband was inaugurated President.
</p><p>On November 22, 1963, Jacqueline was riding with her husband in a presidential motorcade in <a href="/wiki/Dallas" title="Dallas">Dallas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, when he was assassinated. Following <a href="/wiki/State_funeral_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="State funeral of John F. Kennedy">his funeral</a>, she and her children largely withdrew from public view. In 1968, she married Greek shipping magnate <a href="/wiki/Aristotle_Onassis" title="Aristotle Onassis">Aristotle Onassis</a>. Following Onassis's death in 1975, she had a career as a book editor in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>. She died on May 19, 1994, of <a href="/wiki/Non-Hodgkin_lymphoma" title="Non-Hodgkin lymphoma">non-Hodgkin lymphoma</a>, aged 64.
</p><p>During her lifetime, Jacqueline Kennedy was regarded as an international fashion icon.<sup id="cite_ref-Craughwell-Varda1999_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Craughwell-Varda1999-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Her famous ensemble of a <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_pink_Chanel_suit" class="mw-redirect" title="Kennedy pink Chanel suit">pink Chanel suit</a> and matching <a href="/wiki/Pillbox_hat" title="Pillbox hat">pillbox hat</a> that she wore in Dallas has become a symbol of her <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of John F. Kennedy">husband's assassination</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FordMitchell2004_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FordMitchell2004-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Even after her death, she ranks as one of the most popular and recognizable First Ladies, and in 1999 she was listed as one of <a href="/wiki/Gallup%27s_most_admired_man_and_woman_poll" title="Gallup&#39;s most admired man and woman poll">Gallup's Most-Admired Men and Women</a> of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-GallupWomen_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GallupWomen-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span id="Early_life_.281929.E2.80.931951.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_(1929–1951)">Early life (1929–1951)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life (1929–1951)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_and_childhood">Family and childhood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Family and childhood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Jacqueline Lee Bouvier was born on July 28, 1929, at <a href="/wiki/Stony_Brook_Southampton_Hospital" title="Stony Brook Southampton Hospital">Stony Brook Southampton Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/Southampton_(village),_New_York" title="Southampton (village), New York">Southampton, New York</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a> stockbroker <a href="/wiki/John_Vernou_Bouvier_III" title="John Vernou Bouvier III">John Vernou "Black Jack" Bouvier III</a> and socialite <a href="/wiki/Janet_Lee_Bouvier" title="Janet Lee Bouvier">Janet Norton Lee</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Bouvier's mother was of <a href="/wiki/Irish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish American">Irish</a> descent,<sup id="cite_ref-Pottker7_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pottker7-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and her father had <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish American">Scottish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/English_American" class="mw-redirect" title="English American">English</a> ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-Flaherty_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flaherty-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup>
Named after her father, Bouvier was <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptized</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St._Ignatius_Loyola" class="mw-redirect" title="Church of St. Ignatius Loyola">Church of St. Ignatius Loyola</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>; she was raised in the <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> faith.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Her sister <a href="/wiki/Lee_Radziwill" title="Lee Radziwill">Lee</a> was born four years later in 1933.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bouvier spent her early childhood years in Manhattan and at <a href="/wiki/Lasata" title="Lasata">Lasata</a>, the Bouviers' country estate in <a href="/wiki/East_Hampton_(village),_New_York" title="East Hampton (village), New York">East Hampton</a> on <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTobituary_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTobituary-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> She idolized her father, who likewise favored her over her sister, calling his elder child "the most beautiful daughter a man ever had".<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Biographer Tina Flaherty pointed out Jackie's early confidence in herself, seeing a link to her father's praise and positive attitude to her, and her sister Lee has stated that she would not have gained her "independence and individuality" had it not been for the relationship she had with their father and paternal grandfather, <a href="/wiki/John_Vernou_Bouvier_Jr." title="John Vernou Bouvier Jr.">John Vernou Bouvier Jr.</a><sup id="cite_ref-Tracy9_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tracy9-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> From an early age, Bouvier was an enthusiastic <a href="/wiki/Equestrianism" title="Equestrianism">equestrienne</a> who successfully competed in the sport; horse-riding would remain a lifelong passion.<sup id="cite_ref-Tracy9_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tracy9-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jacqueline_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacqueline-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> She also took ballet lessons, was an avid reader, and excelled at learning languages, with French being particularly emphasized in her upbringing.<sup id="cite_ref-Tracy38_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tracy38-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jacqueline_Bouvier_by_David_Berne,_1935.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Jacqueline_Bouvier_by_David_Berne%2C_1935.jpg/180px-Jacqueline_Bouvier_by_David_Berne%2C_1935.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Jacqueline_Bouvier_by_David_Berne%2C_1935.jpg/270px-Jacqueline_Bouvier_by_David_Berne%2C_1935.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Jacqueline_Bouvier_by_David_Berne%2C_1935.jpg/360px-Jacqueline_Bouvier_by_David_Berne%2C_1935.jpg 2x" data-file-width="688" data-file-height="865" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jacqueline_Bouvier_by_David_Berne,_1935.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Six-year-old Bouvier in 1935</div></div></div><p>In 1935, Bouvier was enrolled in Manhattan's <a href="/wiki/Chapin_School_(Manhattan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chapin School (Manhattan)">Chapin School</a>, which she attended for Grades 1–6.<sup id="cite_ref-Jacqueline_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacqueline-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> She was a bright student but often misbehaved; one of her teachers described her as "a darling child, the prettiest little girl, very clever, very artistic, and full of the devil".<sup id="cite_ref-jfklibrary_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfklibrary-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Bouvier's mother attributed her daughter's behavior to the way that she finished her assignments ahead of classmates and then acted out in boredom.<sup id="cite_ref-Harris540_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris540-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Her behavior improved after the headmistress warned her that none of her positive qualities would matter if she did not behave.<sup id="cite_ref-Harris540_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris540-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The marriage of Bouvier's parents was strained by her father's <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Extramarital_affair" class="mw-redirect" title="Extramarital affair">extramarital affairs</a>; the family had also struggled with financial difficulties following the <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Crash_of_1929" title="Wall Street Crash of 1929">Wall Street Crash of 1929</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTobituary_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTobituary-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> They separated in 1936 and divorced four years later, with the press publishing intimate details of the split.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> According to her cousin <a href="/wiki/John_H._Davis_(author)" title="John H. Davis (author)">John H. Davis</a>, Bouvier was deeply affected by the divorce and subsequently had a "tendency to withdraw frequently into a private world of her own".<sup id="cite_ref-NYTobituary_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTobituary-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> When her mother married <a href="/wiki/Standard_Oil" title="Standard Oil">Standard Oil</a> heir <a href="/wiki/Hugh_D._Auchincloss" title="Hugh D. Auchincloss">Hugh Dudley Auchincloss, Jr.</a>,  Bouvier and her sister did not attend the ceremony, because it was arranged quickly and travel was restricted due to <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tracy17_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tracy17-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Bouvier gained three step-siblings from Auchincloss' two previous marriages, Hugh "Yusha" Auchincloss III, Thomas Gore Auchincloss, and <a href="/wiki/Nina_Gore_Auchincloss" class="mw-redirect" title="Nina Gore Auchincloss">Nina Gore Auchincloss</a>; she formed the closest bond with Yusha, who became one of her most trusted confidants.<sup id="cite_ref-Tracy17_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tracy17-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The marriage later produced two more children, <a href="/wiki/Janet_Auchincloss_Rutherfurd" title="Janet Auchincloss Rutherfurd">Janet Jennings Auchincloss</a> in 1945 and James Lee Auchincloss in 1947.
</p><p>After the remarriage, Auchincloss' Merrywood estate in <a href="/wiki/McLean,_Virginia" title="McLean, Virginia">McLean, Virginia</a>, became the Bouvier sisters' primary residence, although they also spent time at his other estate, <a href="/wiki/Hammersmith_Farm" title="Hammersmith Farm">Hammersmith Farm</a> in <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Rhode_Island" title="Newport, Rhode Island">Newport, Rhode Island</a>, and in their father's homes in New York City and Long Island.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTobituary_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTobituary-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Although she retained a relationship with her father, Bouvier also regarded her stepfather as a close paternal figure.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTobituary_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTobituary-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He gave her a stable environment and the pampered childhood she never would have experienced otherwise.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> While Bouvier adjusted to her mother's remarriage, she sometimes felt like an outsider in the <a href="/wiki/White_Anglo-Saxon_Protestant" title="White Anglo-Saxon Protestant">WASP</a> social circle of the Auchinclosses, attributing the feeling to her being Catholic as well as being a child of divorce, which was not common in that social group at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After six years at Chapin, Bouvier attended the <a href="/wiki/Holton-Arms_School" title="Holton-Arms School">Holton-Arms School</a> in Northwest <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> from 1942 to 1944, and <a href="/wiki/Miss_Porter%27s_School" title="Miss Porter&#39;s School">Miss Porter's School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Farmington,_Connecticut" title="Farmington, Connecticut">Farmington, Connecticut</a>, from 1944 to 1947.<sup id="cite_ref-Pottker7_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pottker7-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> She chose Miss Porter's because it was a boarding school that allowed her to distance herself from the Auchinclosses, and because the school placed an emphasis on college preparatory classes.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> In her senior class yearbook, Bouvier was acknowledged for "her wit, her accomplishment as a horsewoman, and her unwillingness to become a housewife". Jacqueline later hired her childhood friend <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Tuckerman" title="Nancy Tuckerman">Nancy Tuckerman</a> to be her <a href="/wiki/White_House_Social_Secretary" title="White House Social Secretary">Social Secretary</a> at the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> She graduated among the top students of her class and received the Maria McKinney Memorial Award for Excellence in Literature.<sup id="cite_ref-Spoto63_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spoto63-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="College_and_early_career">College and early career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: College and early career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the fall of 1947, Bouvier entered <a href="/wiki/Vassar_College" title="Vassar College">Vassar College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Poughkeepsie_(town),_New_York" title="Poughkeepsie (town), New York">Poughkeepsie, New York</a>, which at that time was an all-women's institution.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> She had wanted to attend <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Lawrence_College" title="Sarah Lawrence College">Sarah Lawrence College</a>, closer to New York City, but her parents insisted that she choose the more geographically isolated Vassar.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Bouvier was an accomplished student who participated in the school's art and drama clubs and wrote for its newspaper.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTobituary_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTobituary-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Spoto67_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spoto67-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Due to her dislike for the college, she did not take an active part in its social life and instead traveled back to Manhattan on the weekends.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> She had made her debut to <a href="/wiki/High_society_(group)" class="mw-redirect" title="High society (group)">high society</a> in the summer before entering college and became a frequent presence in New York social functions. Hearst columnist <a href="/wiki/Igor_Cassini" title="Igor Cassini">Igor Cassini</a> dubbed her the "<a href="/wiki/Debutante#United_States" title="Debutante">debutante</a> of the year".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Bouvier spent her junior year (1949–1950) in France—at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Grenoble" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Grenoble">University of Grenoble</a> in <a href="/wiki/Grenoble" title="Grenoble">Grenoble</a>, and at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">Sorbonne</a> in Paris—in a study-abroad program through <a href="/wiki/Smith_College" title="Smith College">Smith College</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Upon returning home, she transferred to <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_University" title="George Washington University">George Washington University</a> in Washington, D.C., graduating with a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Arts" title="Bachelor of Arts">Bachelor of Arts</a> degree in <a href="/wiki/French_literature" title="French literature">French literature</a> in 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-FirstLadies_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FirstLadies-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> During the early years of her marriage to John F. Kennedy, she took continuing education classes in <a href="/wiki/American_history" class="mw-redirect" title="American history">American history</a> at <a href="/wiki/Georgetown_University" title="Georgetown University">Georgetown University</a> in Washington, D.C.<sup id="cite_ref-FirstLadies_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FirstLadies-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While attending George Washington, Bouvier won a twelve-month junior editorship at <i><a href="/wiki/Vogue_(magazine)" title="Vogue (magazine)">Vogue</a></i> magazine; she had been selected over several hundred other women nationwide.<sup id="cite_ref-prixdeparis_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prixdeparis-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The position entailed working for six months in the magazine's New York City office and spending the remaining six months in Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-prixdeparis_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prixdeparis-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Before beginning the job, Bouvier celebrated her college graduation and her sister Lee's high school graduation by traveling with her to Europe for the summer.<sup id="cite_ref-prixdeparis_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prixdeparis-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The trip was the subject of her only autobiography, <i>One Special Summer</i>, co-authored with Lee; it is also the only one of her published works to feature Jacqueline's drawings.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> On her first day at <i>Vogue</i>, the managing editor advised her to quit and go back to Washington. According to biographer <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Leaming" title="Barbara Leaming">Barbara Leaming</a>, the editor was concerned about Bouvier's marriage prospects; she was 22 years of age and was considered too old to be single in her social circles. Bouvier followed the advice, left the job and returned to Washington after only one day of work.<sup id="cite_ref-prixdeparis_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prixdeparis-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bouvier moved back to Merrywood and was hired as a part-time receptionist at the <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Times-Herald" title="Washington Times-Herald">Washington Times-Herald</a></i>. A week later, she approached editor Frank Waldrop and requested more challenging work; she was given the position of "Inquiring Camera Girl", despite Waldrop's initial concerns about her competence.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The position required her to pose witty questions to individuals chosen at random on the street and take their pictures for publication in the newspaper alongside selected quotations from their responses.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTobituary_14-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTobituary-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In addition to the random "<a href="/wiki/Man_on_the_street" class="mw-redirect" title="Man on the street">man on the street</a>" vignettes, she sometimes sought interviews with people of interest, such as six-year-old <a href="/wiki/Tricia_Nixon" class="mw-redirect" title="Tricia Nixon">Tricia Nixon</a>. Bouvier interviewed Tricia a few days after her father <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> was elected to the vice presidency in the <a href="/wiki/1952_United_States_presidential_election" title="1952 United States presidential election">1952 election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> During this time, Bouvier was briefly engaged to a young stockbroker named <a href="/wiki/John_G._W._Husted,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="John G. W. Husted, Jr.">John Husted</a>. After only a month of dating, the couple published the announcement in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> in January 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> After three months, Bouvier called off the engagement because she had found him "immature and boring" once she got to know him better.<sup id="cite_ref-Spoto89_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spoto89-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_to_John_F._Kennedy">Marriage to John F. Kennedy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Marriage to John F. Kennedy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Toni_Frissell,_John_F._Kennedy_and_Jacqueline_Bouvier_on_their_wedding_day,_1953.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Toni_Frissell%2C_John_F._Kennedy_and_Jacqueline_Bouvier_on_their_wedding_day%2C_1953.jpg/220px-Toni_Frissell%2C_John_F._Kennedy_and_Jacqueline_Bouvier_on_their_wedding_day%2C_1953.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Toni_Frissell%2C_John_F._Kennedy_and_Jacqueline_Bouvier_on_their_wedding_day%2C_1953.jpg/330px-Toni_Frissell%2C_John_F._Kennedy_and_Jacqueline_Bouvier_on_their_wedding_day%2C_1953.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Toni_Frissell%2C_John_F._Kennedy_and_Jacqueline_Bouvier_on_their_wedding_day%2C_1953.jpg/440px-Toni_Frissell%2C_John_F._Kennedy_and_Jacqueline_Bouvier_on_their_wedding_day%2C_1953.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4297" data-file-height="4423" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Toni_Frissell,_John_F._Kennedy_and_Jacqueline_Bouvier_on_their_wedding_day,_1953.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Senator <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> and Jacqueline Bouvier Kennedy on their wedding day, September 12, 1953</div></div></div>
<p>Bouvier and <a href="/wiki/U.S._Representative" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Representative">U.S. Representative</a> <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> belonged to the same social circle and were formally introduced by a mutual friend, journalist <a href="/wiki/Charles_L._Bartlett_(journalist)" title="Charles L. Bartlett (journalist)">Charles L. Bartlett</a>, at a dinner party in May 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTobituary_14-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTobituary-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Bouvier was attracted to Kennedy's physical appearance, charm, wit and wealth. The pair also shared the similarities of Catholicism, writing, enjoying reading and having previously lived abroad.<sup id="cite_ref-O_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy was busy running for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_election_in_Massachusetts,_1952" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Senate election in Massachusetts, 1952">U.S. Senate in Massachusetts</a>; the relationship grew more serious and he proposed to her after the November election. Bouvier took some time to accept, because she had been assigned to cover the <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation of Queen Elizabeth II">coronation of Queen Elizabeth II</a> in London for <i>The Washington Times-Herald</i>. After a month in Europe, she returned to the United States and accepted Kennedy's marriage proposal. She then resigned from her position at the newspaper.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Their engagement was officially announced on June 25, 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTEngagement_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTEngagement-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bouvier and Kennedy were married on September 12, 1953, at <a href="/wiki/St._Mary%27s_Church_Complex_(Newport,_Rhode_Island)" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Mary&#39;s Church Complex (Newport, Rhode Island)">St. Mary's Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Rhode_Island" title="Newport, Rhode Island">Newport, Rhode Island</a>, in a <a href="/wiki/Mass_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mass (Catholic Church)">mass</a> celebrated by Boston's Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cushing" title="Richard Cushing">Richard Cushing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The wedding was considered the social event of the season with an estimated 700 guests at the ceremony and 1,200 at the reception that followed at Hammersmith Farm.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wedding_dress_of_Jacqueline_Bouvier" title="Wedding dress of Jacqueline Bouvier">wedding dress</a>, now housed in the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Library" class="mw-redirect" title="Kennedy Library">Kennedy Library</a> in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, Massachusetts, and the dresses of her attendants were created by designer <a href="/wiki/Ann_Lowe" title="Ann Lowe">Ann Lowe</a> of New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_F._Kennedy_after_spinal_surgery_cph.3c33052.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/John_F._Kennedy_after_spinal_surgery_cph.3c33052.jpg/220px-John_F._Kennedy_after_spinal_surgery_cph.3c33052.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="279" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/John_F._Kennedy_after_spinal_surgery_cph.3c33052.jpg/330px-John_F._Kennedy_after_spinal_surgery_cph.3c33052.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/John_F._Kennedy_after_spinal_surgery_cph.3c33052.jpg/440px-John_F._Kennedy_after_spinal_surgery_cph.3c33052.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2778" data-file-height="3528" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_F._Kennedy_after_spinal_surgery_cph.3c33052.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jacqueline Kennedy with her husband, <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>, after his spinal surgery, December 1954</div></div></div>
<p>The newlyweds honeymooned in <a href="/wiki/Acapulco" title="Acapulco">Acapulco</a>, Mexico, before settling in their new home, <a href="/wiki/Hickory_Hill_(McLean,_Virginia)" title="Hickory Hill (McLean, Virginia)">Hickory Hill</a> in McLean, Virginia, a suburb of Washington, D.C.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy developed a warm relationship with her in-laws, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Kennedy" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Kennedy">Joseph</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rose_Kennedy" title="Rose Kennedy">Rose Kennedy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OBrien295_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OBrien295-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mrs.Kennedy31_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mrs.Kennedy31-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In the early years of their marriage, the couple faced several personal setbacks. John suffered from <a href="/wiki/Addison%27s_Disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Addison&#39;s Disease">Addison's Disease</a> and from chronic and at times debilitating back pain, which had been exacerbated by a war injury; in late 1954, he underwent a near-fatal spinal operation.<sup id="cite_ref-Dallek_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dallek-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, Jacqueline suffered a <a href="/wiki/Miscarriage" title="Miscarriage">miscarriage</a> in 1955 and in August 1956 gave birth to a stillborn daughter, Arabella.<sup id="cite_ref-Reference1A_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reference1A-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> They subsequently sold their Hickory Hill estate to John's brother <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Robert F. Kennedy">Robert</a>, who occupied it with his wife <a href="/wiki/Ethel_Kennedy" title="Ethel Kennedy">Ethel</a> and their growing family, and bought a townhouse on N Street in <a href="/wiki/Georgetown,_Washington,_DC" class="mw-redirect" title="Georgetown, Washington, DC">Georgetown</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pottker7_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pottker7-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Jacqueline gave birth to a daughter <a href="/wiki/Caroline_Kennedy" title="Caroline Kennedy">Caroline</a> on November 27, 1957.<sup id="cite_ref-Reference1A_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reference1A-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>  At the time, she and John were campaigning for his re-election to the Senate, and they posed with their infant daughter for the cover of the April 21, 1958 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life Magazine</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (July 2018)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> They traveled together during the campaign, trying to narrow the geographical gap between them that had persisted for the first five years of the marriage. Soon enough, John Kennedy started to notice the value that his wife added to his congressional campaign. <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_O%27Donnell" title="Kenneth O&#39;Donnell">Kenneth O'Donnell</a> remembered that "the size of the crowd was twice as big" when she accompanied her husband; he also recalled her as "always cheerful and obliging". John's mother <a href="/wiki/Rose_Kennedy" title="Rose Kennedy">Rose</a> observed Jacqueline as not being "a natural-born campaigner" due to her shyness and being uncomfortable with too much attention.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In November 1958, John Kennedy was reelected to a second term. He credited Jacqueline's visibility in both ads and stumping as vital assets in securing his victory, and he called her "simply invaluable".<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 1959, historian <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur M. Schlesinger">Arthur M. Schlesinger</a> visited the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Compound" title="Kennedy Compound">Kennedy Compound</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hyannis_Port" class="mw-redirect" title="Hyannis Port">Hyannis Port</a> and had his first conversation with Jacqueline; he found her to have "tremendous awareness, an all-seeing eye and a ruthless judgment".<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> That year, Jack Kennedy traveled to 14 states, with Jacqueline taking long breaks from the trips so she could spend time with their daughter Caroline. She also counseled her husband on improving his wardrobe in preparation for his intended presidential campaign the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In particular, she traveled to <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> to visit Edmund Reggie and to help her husband garner support in the state for his presidential bid.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="First_Lady_of_the_United_States_.281961.E2.80.931963.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Lady_of_the_United_States_(1961–1963)">First Lady of the United States (1961–1963)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: First Lady of the United States (1961–1963)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_for_presidency">Campaign for presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Campaign for presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jfk-appleton.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Jfk-appleton.jpg/220px-Jfk-appleton.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Jfk-appleton.jpg/330px-Jfk-appleton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Jfk-appleton.jpg/440px-Jfk-appleton.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="470" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jfk-appleton.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jacqueline with her husband as he campaigns for the presidency in <a href="/wiki/Appleton,_Wisconsin" title="Appleton, Wisconsin">Appleton, Wisconsin</a>, March 1960</div></div></div>
<p>On January 3, 1960, John F. Kennedy was a United States Senator from Massachusettes when he announced his candidacy for the presidency and launched his campaign nationwide. In the early months of the election year, Jacqueline accompanied her husband to campaign events such as whistle-stops and dinners.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Shortly after the campaign began, she became pregnant. Due to her previous high-risk pregnancies, she decided to stay at home in Georgetown.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Jacqueline subsequently participated in the campaign by writing a weekly syndicated newspaper column, <i>Campaign Wife</i>, answering correspondence, and giving interviews to the media.<sup id="cite_ref-jfklibrary_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfklibrary-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite not participating on the campaign trail, Jacqueline became the subject of intense media attention with her fashion choices.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> On one hand, she was admired for her personal style; she was frequently featured in women's magazines alongside film stars and named as one of the 12 best-dressed women in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Beasleyfashion_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beasleyfashion-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, her preference for French designers and her spending on her wardrobe brought her negative press.<sup id="cite_ref-Beasleyfashion_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beasleyfashion-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In order to downplay her wealthy background, Jacqueline stressed the amount of work she was doing for the campaign and declined to publicly discuss her clothing choices.<sup id="cite_ref-Beasleyfashion_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beasleyfashion-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On July 13 at the <a href="/wiki/1960_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1960 Democratic National Convention">1960 Democratic National Convention</a> in Los Angeles, the party nominated John F. Kennedy for President of the United States. Jacqueline did not attend the nomination due to her pregnancy, which had been publicly announced ten days earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-Spoto155_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spoto155-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> She was in Hyannis Port when she watched the September 26, 1960 debate—which was the nation's first televised presidential debate—between her husband and Republican candidate <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a>, who was the incumbent Vice President. Marian Cannon, the wife of Arthur Schlesinger, watched the debate with her. Days after the debates, Jacqueline contacted Schlesinger and informed him that John wanted his aid along with that of <a href="/wiki/John_Kenneth_Galbraith" title="John Kenneth Galbraith">John Kenneth Galbraith</a> in preparing for the third debate on October 13; she wished for them to give her husband new ideas and speeches.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> On September 29, 1960, the Kennedys appeared together for a joint interview on <i><a href="/wiki/Person_to_Person" title="Person to Person">Person to Person</a></i>, interviewed by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Collingwood_(journalist)" title="Charles Collingwood (journalist)">Charles Collingwood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Spoto155_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spoto155-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_First_Lady">As First Lady</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: As First Lady">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JBKJFKMalraux.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/JBKJFKMalraux.jpg/220px-JBKJFKMalraux.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/JBKJFKMalraux.jpg/330px-JBKJFKMalraux.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/JBKJFKMalraux.jpg/440px-JBKJFKMalraux.jpg 2x" data-file-width="647" data-file-height="652" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JBKJFKMalraux.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy, President John F. Kennedy, <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Malraux" title="André Malraux">André Malraux</a>, Marie-Madeleine Lioux Malraux, <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lady_Bird_Johnson" title="Lady Bird Johnson">Lady Bird Johnson</a> prior to a dinner, April 1962. The First Lady is wearing a gown designed by <a href="/wiki/Oleg_Cassini" title="Oleg Cassini">Oleg Cassini</a><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>On November 8, 1960, John F. Kennedy narrowly defeated Republican opponent <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1960_United_States_presidential_election" title="1960 United States presidential election">U.S. presidential election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jfklibrary_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfklibrary-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> A little over two weeks later on November 25, Jacqueline gave birth to the couple's first son, <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="John F. Kennedy, Jr.">John F. Kennedy, Jr.</a><sup id="cite_ref-jfklibrary_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfklibrary-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> She spent two weeks recuperating in the hospital, during which the most minute details of both her and her son's conditions were reported by the media in what has been considered the first instance of national interest in the Kennedy family.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Her husband was sworn in as president on January 20, 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-jfklibrary_21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfklibrary-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> As a presidential couple, the Kennedys differed from the Eisenhowers by their political affiliation, youth, and their relationship with the media. Historian <a href="/wiki/Gil_Troy" title="Gil Troy">Gil Troy</a> has noted that in particular, they "emphasized vague appearances rather than specific accomplishments or passionate commitments" and therefore fit in well in the early 1960s' "cool, TV-oriented culture".<sup id="cite_ref-Beasley,_p._76_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beasley,_p._76-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The discussion about Jacqueline's fashion choices continued during her years in the White House, and she became a trendsetter, hiring American designer <a href="/wiki/Oleg_Cassini" title="Oleg Cassini">Oleg Cassini</a> to design her wardrobe.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> She was the first presidential wife to hire a press secretary, <a href="/wiki/Pamela_Turnure" title="Pamela Turnure">Pamela Turnure</a>, and carefully managed her contact with the media, usually shying away from making public statements, and strictly controlling the extent to which her children were photographed.<sup id="cite_ref-firstladies1_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-firstladies1-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bimage_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bimage-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The media portrayed Kennedy as the ideal woman, which led academic Maurine Beasley to observe that she "created an unrealistic media expectation for first ladies that would challenge her successors".<sup id="cite_ref-bimage_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bimage-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, the First Lady attracted worldwide positive public attention and gained allies for the White House and international support for the Kennedy administration and its <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> policies.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Jacqueline stated that her priority as a First Lady was to take care of the President and their children, she also dedicated her time to the promotion of American arts and preservation of its history.<sup id="cite_ref-jfklibrarytwo_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfklibrarytwo-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-miller_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>  The restoration of the White House was her main contribution, but she also furthered the cause by hosting social events that brought together elite figures from politics and the arts.<sup id="cite_ref-jfklibrarytwo_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfklibrarytwo-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-miller_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> One of her unrealized goals was to found a Department of the Arts, but she did contribute to the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/National_Endowment_for_the_Arts" title="National Endowment for the Arts">National Endowment for the Arts</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Endowment_for_the_Humanities" title="National Endowment for the Humanities">National Endowment for the Humanities</a>, established during Johnson's tenure.<sup id="cite_ref-miller_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="White_House_restoration">White House restoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: White House restoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Jacqueline had visited the White House on two occasions before she became First Lady: the first time as a grade-school tourist in 1941 and again as the guest of outgoing First Lady <a href="/wiki/Mamie_Eisenhower" title="Mamie Eisenhower">Mamie Eisenhower</a> shortly before her husband's inauguration.<sup id="cite_ref-jfklibrarytwo_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfklibrarytwo-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> She was dismayed to find that the mansion's rooms were furnished with undistinguished pieces that displayed little historical significance<sup id="cite_ref-jfklibrarytwo_83-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfklibrarytwo-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> and made it her first major project as First Lady to restore its historical character. On her first day in residence, she began her efforts with the help of interior decorator <a href="/wiki/Sister_Parish" title="Sister Parish">Sister Parish</a>. She decided to make the family quarters attractive and suitable for family life by adding a kitchen on the family floor and new rooms for her children. The $50,000 that had been appropriated for this effort was almost immediately exhausted. Continuing the project, she established a fine arts committee to oversee and fund the restoration process and solicited the advice of early American furniture expert <a href="/wiki/Henry_Francis_du_Pont" title="Henry Francis du Pont">Henry du Pont</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jfklibrarytwo_83-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfklibrarytwo-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> To solve the funding problem, a White House guidebook was published, sales of which were used for the restoration.<sup id="cite_ref-jfklibrarytwo_83-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfklibrarytwo-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Working with <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Lambert_Mellon" title="Rachel Lambert Mellon">Rachel Lambert Mellon</a>, Kennedy also oversaw the redesign and replanting of the <a href="/wiki/White_House_Rose_Garden" title="White House Rose Garden">Rose Garden</a> and the East Garden, which was renamed the <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Kennedy_Garden" title="Jacqueline Kennedy Garden">Jacqueline Kennedy Garden</a> after her husband's assassination. In addition, Kennedy helped to stop the destruction of historic homes in <a href="/wiki/President%27s_Park" title="President&#39;s Park">Lafayette Square</a> in Washington, D.C., because she felt these buildings were an important part of the nation's capital and played an essential role in its history.<sup id="cite_ref-jfklibrarytwo_83-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfklibrarytwo-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Collingwood_murrow27s_boys.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Charles_Collingwood_murrow27s_boys.jpg/220px-Charles_Collingwood_murrow27s_boys.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3b/Charles_Collingwood_murrow27s_boys.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="325" data-file-height="301" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Collingwood_murrow27s_boys.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy with <a href="/wiki/Charles_Collingwood_(journalist)" title="Charles Collingwood (journalist)">Charles Collingwood</a> during <a href="/wiki/A_Tour_of_the_White_House_with_Mrs._John_F._Kennedy" title="A Tour of the White House with Mrs. John F. Kennedy">their televised tour</a> of the restored <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> in 1962.</div></div></div><p>Prior to Kennedy's years as First Lady, presidents and their families had taken furnishings and other items from the White House when they departed; this led to the lack of original historical pieces in the mansion.  She personally wrote to possible donors in order to track down these missing furnishings and other historical pieces of interest.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy initiated a Congressional bill establishing that White House furnishings would be the property of the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a> rather than available to departing ex-presidents to claim as their own. She also founded the <a href="/wiki/White_House_Historical_Association" title="White House Historical Association">White House Historical Association</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Committee_for_the_Preservation_of_the_White_House" title="Committee for the Preservation of the White House">Committee for the Preservation of the White House</a>, the position of a permanent <a href="/wiki/Curator_of_the_White_House" class="mw-redirect" title="Curator of the White House">Curator of the White House</a>, the <a href="/wiki/White_House_Endowment_Trust" title="White House Endowment Trust">White House Endowment Trust</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/White_House_Acquisition_Trust" title="White House Acquisition Trust">White House Acquisition Trust</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DesigningCamelot_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DesigningCamelot-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> She was the first presidential spouse to hire a White House curator.<sup id="cite_ref-firstladies1_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-firstladies1-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On February 14, 1962, Jacqueline, accompanied by Charles Collingwood of <a href="/wiki/CBS_News" title="CBS News">CBS News</a>, took American television viewers <a href="/wiki/A_Tour_of_the_White_House_with_Mrs._John_F._Kennedy" title="A Tour of the White House with Mrs. John F. Kennedy">on a tour of the White House</a>. In the tour, she stated that "I feel so strongly that the White House should have as fine a collection of American pictures as possible. It's so important ... the setting in which the presidency is presented to the world, to foreign visitors. The American people should be proud of it. We have such a great civilization. So many foreigners don't realize it. I think this house should be the place we see them best."<sup id="cite_ref-DesigningCamelot_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DesigningCamelot-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The film was watched by 56 million television viewers in the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-jfklibrarytwo_83-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfklibrarytwo-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> and was later distributed to 106 countries. Kennedy won a special <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Television_Arts_%26_Sciences" title="Academy of Television Arts &amp; Sciences">Academy of Television Arts &amp; Sciences</a> Trustees Award for it at the <a href="/wiki/Emmy_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="Emmy Awards">Emmy Awards</a> in 1962, which was accepted on her behalf by <a href="/wiki/Lady_Bird_Johnson" title="Lady Bird Johnson">Lady Bird Johnson</a>. Kennedy was the only First Lady to win an Emmy.<sup id="cite_ref-firstladies1_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-firstladies1-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_trips">Foreign trips</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Foreign trips">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jacqueline_Kennedy_at_the_Taj_Mahal,_15_March_1962.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Jacqueline_Kennedy_at_the_Taj_Mahal%2C_15_March_1962.jpg/220px-Jacqueline_Kennedy_at_the_Taj_Mahal%2C_15_March_1962.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Jacqueline_Kennedy_at_the_Taj_Mahal%2C_15_March_1962.jpg/330px-Jacqueline_Kennedy_at_the_Taj_Mahal%2C_15_March_1962.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Jacqueline_Kennedy_at_the_Taj_Mahal%2C_15_March_1962.jpg/440px-Jacqueline_Kennedy_at_the_Taj_Mahal%2C_15_March_1962.jpg 2x" data-file-width="647" data-file-height="633" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jacqueline_Kennedy_at_the_Taj_Mahal,_15_March_1962.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy at the <a href="/wiki/Taj_Mahal" title="Taj Mahal">Taj Mahal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agra" title="Agra">Agra</a>, Uttar Pradesh, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, March 1962</div></div></div>
<p>Throughout her husband's presidency and more than any of the preceding First Ladies, Kennedy made many official visits to other countries, on her own or with the President.<sup id="cite_ref-FirstLadies_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FirstLadies-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Despite the initial worry that she might not have "political appeal", she proved popular among international dignitaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Beasley,_p._76_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beasley,_p._76-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Before the Kennedys' first official visit to France in 1961, a television special was shot in French with the First Lady on the White House lawn. After arriving in the country, she impressed the public with her ability to speak French, as well as her extensive knowledge of French history.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodman_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> At the conclusion of the visit, <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine seemed delighted with the First Lady and noted, "There was also that fellow who came with her." Even President Kennedy joked, "I am the man who accompanied Jacqueline Kennedy to Paris&#160;– and I have enjoyed it!"<sup id="cite_ref-Time060961_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Time060961-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From France, the Kennedys traveled to Vienna, Austria, where <a href="/wiki/Premier_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Premier of the Soviet Union">Soviet Premier</a> <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a> was asked to shake the President's hand for a photo. He replied, "I'd like to shake her hand first."<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Khrushchev later sent her a puppy; the animal was significant for being the offspring of <a href="/wiki/Soviet_space_dogs" title="Soviet space dogs">Strelka</a>, the dog that had gone to space during a Soviet space mission.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the urging of U.S. Ambassador to India <a href="/wiki/John_Kenneth_Galbraith" title="John Kenneth Galbraith">John Kenneth Galbraith</a>, Kennedy undertook a tour of India and Pakistan with her sister Lee Radziwill in 1962. The tour was amply documented in photojournalism as well as in Galbraith's journals and memoirs. The President of Pakistan, <a href="/wiki/Ayub_Khan_(President_of_Pakistan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayub Khan (President of Pakistan)">Ayub Khan</a>, had given her a horse named Sardar as a gift. He had found out on his visit to the White House that he and the First Lady had a common interest in horses.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> <i>Life</i> magazine correspondent Anne Chamberlin wrote that Kennedy "conducted herself magnificently" although noting that her crowds were smaller than those that President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_Eisenhower" class="mw-redirect" title="Dwight Eisenhower">Dwight Eisenhower</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> attracted when they had previously visited these countries.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In addition to these well-publicized trips during the three years of the Kennedy administration, she traveled to countries including Afghanistan, Austria, Canada,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Colombia, United Kingdom, Greece, Italy, Mexico,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Morocco, Turkey, and Venezuela.<sup id="cite_ref-FirstLadies_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FirstLadies-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Unlike her husband, Kennedy was fluent in Spanish, which she used to address Latin American audiences.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_of_infant_son">Death of infant son</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Death of infant son">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Bouvier_Kennedy" title="Patrick Bouvier Kennedy">Patrick Bouvier Kennedy</a></div>
<p>In early 1963, Jacqueline was again pregnant, which led her to curtail her official duties. She spent most of the summer at a home she and the President had rented on Squaw Island, which was near the Kennedy compound on <a href="/wiki/Cape_Cod" title="Cape Cod">Cape Cod, Massachusetts</a>. On August 7 (five weeks ahead of her scheduled due date), she went into labor and gave birth to a boy, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Bouvier_Kennedy" title="Patrick Bouvier Kennedy">Patrick Bouvier Kennedy</a>, via emergency Caesarean section at nearby <a href="/wiki/Otis_Air_Force_Base" class="mw-redirect" title="Otis Air Force Base">Otis Air Force Base</a>. The infant's lungs were not fully developed, and he was transferred from Cape Cod to <a href="/wiki/Boston_Children%27s_Hospital" title="Boston Children&#39;s Hospital">Boston Children's Hospital</a>, where he died of <a href="/wiki/Hyaline_membrane_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Hyaline membrane disease">hyaline membrane disease</a> two days after birth.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Jacqueline had remained at Otis Air Force Base to recuperate after the Caesarean delivery; her husband went to Boston to be with their infant son and was present when he died. On August 14, the President returned to Otis to take her home and gave an impromptu speech to thank nurses and airmen who had gathered in her suite. In appreciation, she presented hospital staff with framed and signed lithographs of the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-VanityFair_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VanityFair-99">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The First Lady was deeply affected by Patrick's death<sup id="cite_ref-Levingston2013_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levingston2013-100">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> and proceeded to enter a state of <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Leaming120_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leaming120-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> However, the loss of their child had a positive impact on the marriage and brought the couple closer together in their shared grief.<sup id="cite_ref-Levingston2013_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levingston2013-100">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Arthur Schlesinger wrote that while President Kennedy always "regarded Jacqueline with genuine affection and pride," their marriage "never seemed more solid than in the later months of 1963".<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Jacqueline's friend <a href="/wiki/Aristotle_Onassis" title="Aristotle Onassis">Aristotle Onassis</a> was aware of her depression and invited her to his yacht to recuperate. President Kennedy initially had reservations, but he relented because he believed that it would be "good for her". The trip was widely disapproved of within the Kennedy administration, by much of the general public, and in Congress. The First Lady returned to the United States on October 17, 1963. She would later say she regretted being away as long as she was but had been "melancholy after the death of my baby".<sup id="cite_ref-Leaming120_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leaming120-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Assassination_and_funeral_of_John_F._Kennedy">Assassination and funeral of John F. Kennedy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Assassination and funeral of John F. Kennedy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of John F. Kennedy">Assassination of John F. Kennedy</a>, <a href="/wiki/State_funeral_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="State funeral of John F. Kennedy">State funeral of John F. Kennedy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_autopsy" title="John F. Kennedy autopsy">John F. Kennedy autopsy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_F._Kennedy_motorcade,_Dallas_crop.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/John_F._Kennedy_motorcade%2C_Dallas_crop.png/220px-John_F._Kennedy_motorcade%2C_Dallas_crop.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/John_F._Kennedy_motorcade%2C_Dallas_crop.png/330px-John_F._Kennedy_motorcade%2C_Dallas_crop.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/John_F._Kennedy_motorcade%2C_Dallas_crop.png/440px-John_F._Kennedy_motorcade%2C_Dallas_crop.png 2x" data-file-width="3641" data-file-height="2444" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_F._Kennedy_motorcade,_Dallas_crop.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The President and First Lady  in the rear seat of the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_State_Car_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Presidential State Car (United States)">Presidential limousine</a> minutes before the assassination</div></div></div>
<p>On November 21, 1963, the First Lady and the President embarked on a political trip to Texas with several goals in mind; this was the first time that she had joined her husband on such a trip in the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> After a breakfast on November 22, they took a very short flight on <a href="/wiki/VC-137C_SAM_26000" title="VC-137C SAM 26000">Air Force One</a> from Fort Worth's <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_Joint_Reserve_Base_Fort_Worth" title="Naval Air Station Joint Reserve Base Fort Worth">Carswell Air Force Base</a> to Dallas' <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Love_Field" title="Dallas Love Field">Love Field</a>, accompanied by Texas Governor <a href="/wiki/John_Connally" title="John Connally">John Connally</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Nellie_Connally" title="Nellie Connally">Nellie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bugliosi_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bugliosi-104">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The First Lady was wearing a <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_pink_Chanel_suit" class="mw-redirect" title="Kennedy pink Chanel suit">bright pink Chanel suit</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Pillbox_hat" title="Pillbox hat">pillbox hat</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Craughwell-Varda1999_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Craughwell-Varda1999-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FordMitchell2004_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FordMitchell2004-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> which had been personally selected by President Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> A 9.5-mile (15.3&#160;km) <a href="/wiki/Motorcade" title="Motorcade">motorcade</a> was to take them to the <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Market_Center" title="Dallas Market Center">Trade Mart</a>, where the president was scheduled to speak at a lunch. The First Lady was seated to her husband's left in the third row of seats in the <a href="/wiki/SS-100-X" title="SS-100-X">presidential limousine</a>, with the Governor and his wife seated in front of them. Vice President <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> and his wife followed in another car in the motorcade.
</p><p>After the motorcade turned the corner onto Elm Street in <a href="/wiki/Dealey_Plaza" title="Dealey Plaza">Dealey Plaza</a>, the First Lady heard what she thought to be a motorcycle <a href="/wiki/Back-fire" title="Back-fire">backfiring</a> and did not realize that it was a gunshot until she heard Governor Connally scream. Within 8.4 seconds, two more shots had rung out, and one of the shots struck her husband in the head. Almost immediately, she began to climb onto the back of the limousine; Secret Service agent <a href="/wiki/Clint_Hill_(Secret_Service)" title="Clint Hill (Secret Service)">Clint Hill</a> later told the <a href="/wiki/Warren_Commission" title="Warren Commission">Warren Commission</a> that he thought she had been reaching across the trunk for a piece of her husband's skull that had been blown off.<sup id="cite_ref-cjhill_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cjhill-106">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Hill ran to the car and leapt onto it, directing her back to her seat. As Hill stood on the back bumper, <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> photographer <a href="/wiki/Ike_Altgens" title="Ike Altgens">Ike Altgens</a> snapped a photograph that was featured on the front pages of newspapers around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-PotP_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PotP-107">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> She would later testify that she saw pictures "of me climbing out the back. But I don't remember that at all".<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office,_November_1963.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg/220px-Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg/330px-Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg/440px-Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office%2C_November_1963.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2374" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lyndon_B._Johnson_taking_the_oath_of_office,_November_1963.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jacqueline, still wearing her blood-stained <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_pink_Chanel_suit" class="mw-redirect" title="Kennedy pink Chanel suit">pink Chanel suit</a>, stands alongside Lyndon B. Johnson as he takes the Presidential oath of office administered by <a href="/wiki/Sarah_T._Hughes" title="Sarah T. Hughes">Sarah Hughes</a> aboard <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_One" title="Air Force One">Air Force One</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The President was rushed for the 3.8 mile trip to <a href="/wiki/Parkland_Hospital" class="mw-redirect" title="Parkland Hospital">Parkland Hospital</a>. At the First Lady's request, she was allowed to be present in the operating room.<sup id="cite_ref-Manchester_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manchester-109">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (November 2015)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> After Jacqueline's husband was pronounced dead, she refused to remove her blood-stained clothing and reportedly regretted having washed the blood off her face and hands, explaining to <a href="/wiki/Lady_Bird_Johnson" title="Lady Bird Johnson">Lady Bird Johnson</a> that she wanted "them to see what they have done to Jack".<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> She continued to wear the blood-stained pink suit as she boarded Air Force One and stood next to Johnson when he took the oath of office as President. The unlaundered suit was donated to the <a href="/wiki/National_Archives_and_Records_Administration" title="National Archives and Records Administration">National Archives and Records Administration</a> in 1964 and, under the terms of an agreement with her daughter <a href="/wiki/Caroline_Kennedy" title="Caroline Kennedy">Caroline</a>, will not be placed on public display until 2103.<sup id="cite_ref-Smart_Pink_Suit,_Preserved_in_Memory_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smart_Pink_Suit,_Preserved_in_Memory-111">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Johnson's biographer Robert Caro wrote that Johnson wanted Jacqueline to be present at his swearing-in in order to demonstrate the legitimacy of his presidency to JFK loyalists and to the world at large.<sup id="cite_ref-caro_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-caro-112">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral,_1963.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral%2C_1963.jpg/220px-JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral%2C_1963.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral%2C_1963.jpg/330px-JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral%2C_1963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral%2C_1963.jpg/440px-JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral%2C_1963.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2954" data-file-height="2336" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral,_1963.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Family members depart the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Capitol" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Capitol">U.S. Capitol</a> after a lying-in-state service for the President, November 24, 1963</div></div></div>
<p>Jacqueline took an active role in planning <a href="/wiki/State_funeral_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="State funeral of John F. Kennedy">her husband's state funeral</a>, modeling it after <a href="/wiki/Funeral_and_burial_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="Funeral and burial of Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>'s service.<sup id="cite_ref-Campbell_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-113">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> She requested a closed casket, overruling the wishes of her brother-in-law, Robert.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The funeral service was held at the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_St._Matthew_the_Apostle" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral of St. Matthew the Apostle">Cathedral of St. Matthew the Apostle</a> in Washington D.C., with the burial taking place at nearby <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="Arlington National Cemetery">Arlington National Cemetery</a>. Jacqueline led the procession on foot and lit the eternal flame—created at her request—at the gravesite. <a href="/wiki/Jeanne_Campbell" title="Jeanne Campbell">Lady Jeanne Campbell</a> reported back to <i><a href="/wiki/The_London_Evening_Standard" class="mw-redirect" title="The London Evening Standard">The London Evening Standard</a></i>: "Jacqueline Kennedy has given the American people ... one thing they have always lacked: Majesty."<sup id="cite_ref-Campbell_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-113">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A week after the assassination,<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> new president Lyndon B. Johnson issued an <a href="/wiki/Executive_order" title="Executive order">executive order</a> that established the eponymous <a href="/wiki/Warren_Commission" title="Warren Commission">Warren Commission</a>—led by <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/Earl_Warren" title="Earl Warren">Earl Warren</a>—to investigate the assassination.
