Ten months later, the Commission issued its report with the controversial finding that <a href="/wiki/Lee_Harvey_Oswald" title="Lee Harvey Oswald">Lee Harvey Oswald</a> had acted alone when he assassinated President Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Privately, his widow cared little about the investigation, stating that even if they had the right suspect, it would not bring her husband back.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, she gave a deposition to the Warren Commission.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> Following the assassination and the media coverage that had focused intensely on her during and after the burial, Jacqueline stepped back from official public view, apart from a brief appearance in Washington to honor the Secret Service agent, Clint Hill, who had climbed aboard the limousine in Dallas to try to shield her and the President.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span id="Life_following_the_assassination_.281963.E2.80.931975.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_following_the_assassination_(1963–1975)">Life following the assassination (1963–1975)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Life following the assassination (1963–1975)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mourning_period_and_later_public_appearances">Mourning period and later public appearances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mourning period and later public appearances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:15em;&#32;; background-color: #CCDDFF;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r887775652">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">Don't let it be forgot, that once there was a spot, for one brief, shining moment that was known as Camelot.
There'll be great presidents again ... but there will never be another Camelot.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup></div>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">—Kennedy describing the years of her husband's presidency for <i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life</a></i></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>On  November 29, 1963—a week after her husband's assassination—Jacqueline was interviewed in <a href="/wiki/Hyannis_Port" class="mw-redirect" title="Hyannis Port">Hyannis Port</a> by <a href="/wiki/Theodore_H._White" title="Theodore H. White">Theodore H. White</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life</a></i> magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> In that session, she famously compared the Kennedy years in the White House to <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a>'s mythical <a href="/wiki/Camelot" title="Camelot">Camelot</a>, commenting that the President often played the title song of <a href="/wiki/Lerner_and_Loewe" title="Lerner and Loewe">Lerner and Loewe</a>'s musical recording before retiring to bed. She also quoted <a href="/wiki/Guinevere" title="Guinevere">Queen Guinevere</a> from the musical, trying to express how the loss felt.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The era of the Kennedy administration would subsequently often be referred to as the "Camelot Era," although historians have later argued that the comparison is not appropriate, with <a href="/wiki/Robert_Dallek" title="Robert Dallek">Robert Dallek</a> stating that Kennedy's "effort to lionize [her husband] must have provided a therapeutic shield against immobilizing grief."<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy and her children remained in the White House for two weeks following the assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt12071963_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt12071963-125">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Wanting to "do something nice for Jackie," President Johnson offered an <a href="/wiki/U.S._Ambassador_to_France" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Ambassador to France">ambassadorship to France</a> to her, aware of her heritage and fondness for the country's culture, but she turned the offer down, as well as follow-up offers of ambassadorships to <a href="/wiki/U.S._Ambassador_to_Mexico" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Ambassador to Mexico">Mexico</a> and the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Ambassador_to_Great_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Ambassador to Great Britain">United Kingdom</a>. At her request, Johnson renamed the <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> space center the <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_Space_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="John F. Kennedy Space Center">John F. Kennedy Space Center</a> a week after the assassination. Kennedy later publicly praised Johnson for his kindness to her.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy spent 1964 in mourning and made few public appearances. It has been speculated that she may have been suffering from undiagnosed <a href="/wiki/Posttraumatic_stress_disorder" title="Posttraumatic stress disorder">Posttraumatic stress disorder</a> that was due to intrusive flashbacks.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYTobituary_14-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTobituary-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Adler_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adler-129">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In the winter following the assassination, she and the children stayed at <a href="/wiki/Averell_Harriman" class="mw-redirect" title="Averell Harriman">Averell Harriman</a>'s home in Georgetown. On January 14, 1964, Kennedy made a televised appearance from the office of the Attorney General, thanking the public for the "hundreds of thousands of messages" she had received since the assassination and said she had been sustained by America's affection for her late husband.<sup id="cite_ref-Spoto239_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spoto239-130">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> She purchased a house for herself and her children in Georgetown but sold it later in 1964 and bought a 15th-floor penthouse apartment for $250,000 at 1040 <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Avenue" title="Fifth Avenue">Fifth Avenue</a> in Manhattan in the hopes of having more privacy.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the following years, Jacqueline attended selected memorial dedications to her late husband.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> She also oversaw the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_Presidential_Library_and_Museum" title="John F. Kennedy Presidential Library and Museum">John F. Kennedy Presidential Library and Museum</a>, which is the repository for official papers of the Kennedy Administration.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Designed by architect <a href="/wiki/I.M._Pei" class="mw-redirect" title="I.M. Pei">I.M. Pei</a>, it is situated next to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Massachusetts" title="University of Massachusetts">University of Massachusetts</a> campus in Boston.
</p><p>Despite having commissioned <a href="/wiki/William_Manchester" title="William Manchester">William Manchester's</a> authorized account of President Kennedy's death, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Death_of_a_President" title="The Death of a President">The Death of a President</a></i>, Jacqueline was subject to significant media attention in 1966–1967 when she and Robert Kennedy tried to block the publication.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> They sued publishers <a href="/wiki/Harper_%26_Row" class="mw-redirect" title="Harper &amp; Row">Harper &amp; Row</a> in December 1966; the suit was settled the following year when Manchester removed passages that detailed President Kennedy's private life. White viewed the ordeal as validation of the measures the Kennedy family, Jacqueline in particular, were prepared to take to preserve President Kennedy's public image.
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a> in November 1967, <i>Life</i> magazine dubbed Kennedy "America's unofficial roving ambassador" when she and <a href="/wiki/David_Ormsby-Gore,_5th_Baron_Harlech" title="David Ormsby-Gore, 5th Baron Harlech">David Ormsby-Gore</a>, former British ambassador to the United States during the Kennedy administration, traveled to Cambodia, where they visited the religious complex of <a href="/wiki/Angkor_Wat" title="Angkor Wat">Angkor Wat</a> with Chief of State <a href="/wiki/Norodom_Sihanouk" title="Norodom Sihanouk">Norodom Sihanouk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Alam32_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alam32-143">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> According to historian Milton Osbourne, her visit was "the start of the repair to Cambodian-US relations, which had been at a very low ebb".<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> She also attended the <a href="/wiki/Funeral_of_Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Funeral of Martin Luther King, Jr.">funeral services of Martin Luther King, Jr.</a> in <a href="/wiki/Atlanta,_Georgia" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlanta, Georgia">Atlanta, Georgia</a>, in April 1968, despite her initial reluctancy due to the crowds and reminders of President Kennedy's death.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_Robert_F._Kennedy">Relationship with Robert F. Kennedy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Relationship with Robert F. Kennedy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After her husband's assassination, Jacqueline relied heavily on her brother-in-law <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Robert F. Kennedy">Robert F. Kennedy</a>; she observed him to be the "least like his father" of the Kennedy brothers.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> He had been a source of support after she had suffered a miscarriage early in her marriage; it was he, not her husband, who stayed with her in the hospital.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> In the aftermath of the assassination, Bobby became a surrogate father for her children until eventual demands by his own large family and his responsibilities as Attorney General required him to reduce attention.<sup id="cite_ref-Spoto239_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spoto239-130">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> He credited Jackie with convincing him to stay in politics, and she supported his 1964 run for United States Senator from New York.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The January 1968 <a href="/wiki/Tet_offensive" class="mw-redirect" title="Tet offensive">Tet offensive</a> in Vietnam resulted in a drop in President Johnson's poll numbers, and Robert Kennedy's advisors urged him to enter the upcoming presidential race. When <a href="/wiki/Art_Buchwald" title="Art Buchwald">Art Buchwald</a> asked him if he intended to run, Robert replied, "That depends on what Jackie wants me to do".<sup id="cite_ref-FlyntEisenbach_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlyntEisenbach-149">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (July 2018)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> She met with him around this time and encouraged him to run after she had previously advised him to not follow Jack, but to "be yourself". Privately, she worried about his safety; she believed that Bobby was more disliked than her husband had been and that there was "so much hatred" in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> She confided in him about these feelings, but by her own account, he was "fatalistic" like her.<sup id="cite_ref-FlyntEisenbach_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlyntEisenbach-149">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Despite her concerns, Jacqueline campaigned for her brother-in-law and supported him,<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> and at one point even showed outright optimism that through his victory, members of the Kennedy family would once again occupy the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-FlyntEisenbach_149-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlyntEisenbach-149">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Just after midnight <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Time_Zone" title="Pacific Time Zone">PDT</a> on June 5, 1968, an enraged Palestinian gunman named <a href="/wiki/Sirhan_Sirhan" title="Sirhan Sirhan">Sirhan Sirhan</a> <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of Robert F. Kennedy">mortally wounded</a> Robert Kennedy minutes after he and a crowd of his supporters had been celebrating his victory in the California Democratic presidential primary.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTjune61968_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTjune61968-153">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Jacqueline Kennedy rushed to <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> to join his wife Ethel, her brother-in-law <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kennedy" title="Ted Kennedy">Ted Kennedy</a>, and the other Kennedy family members at his hospital bedside. Bobby Kennedy never regained consciousness and died 26 hours after the shooting.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTsirhan_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTsirhan-154">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_to_Aristotle_Onassis">Marriage to Aristotle Onassis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Marriage to Aristotle Onassis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After Robert Kennedy's death, Jacqueline reportedly suffered a relapse of the depression she had suffered in the days following her husband's assassination nearly five years prior.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> She came to fear for her life and those of her children, saying: "If they're killing Kennedys, then my children are targets ... I want to get out of this country".<sup id="cite_ref-jfkjr_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfkjr-156">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On October 20, 1968, Kennedy married her long-time friend <a href="/wiki/Aristotle_Onassis" title="Aristotle Onassis">Aristotle Onassis</a>, a wealthy Greek shipping magnate who was able to provide the privacy and security she sought for herself and her children.<sup id="cite_ref-jfkjr_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfkjr-156">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> The wedding took place on <a href="/wiki/Skorpios" title="Skorpios">Skorpios</a>, Onassis' private Greek island in the <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Sea" title="Ionian Sea">Ionian Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> After marrying Onassis, she took the legal name Jacqueline Onassis and consequently lost her right to Secret Service protection, which is an entitlement of a widow of a U.S. president. The marriage brought her considerable adverse publicity. The fact that Aristotle was divorced and his former wife <a href="/wiki/Tina_Onassis_Niarchos" title="Tina Onassis Niarchos">Athina Livanos</a> was still living led to speculation that Jacqueline might be <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunicated</a> by the Roman Catholic church, though that concern was explicitly dismissed by <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Boston" class="mw-redirect" title="Archbishop of Boston">Boston's Archbishop</a>, Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cushing" title="Richard Cushing">Richard Cushing</a> as "nonsense".<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> She was condemned by some as a "public sinner,"<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> and became the target of <a href="/wiki/Paparazzi" title="Paparazzi">paparazzi</a> who followed her everywhere and nicknamed her "Jackie O".<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During their marriage, the couple inhabited six different residences: her 15-room <a href="/wiki/1040_Fifth_Avenue" title="1040 Fifth Avenue">Fifth Avenue apartment</a> in Manhattan, her horse farm in New Jersey, his Avenue Foch apartment in Paris, his private island Skorpios, his house in Athens, and his yacht <i><a href="/wiki/Christina_O" title="Christina O">Christina O</a></i>. Kennedy ensured that her children continued a connection with the Kennedy family by having Ted Kennedy visit them often.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (July 2018)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> She developed a close relationship with Ted, and from then on he was involved in her public appearances.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Aristotle Onassis' health deteriorated rapidly following the death of his son <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Onassis" title="Alexander Onassis">Alexander</a> in a plane crash in 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> He died of respiratory failure at age 69 in Paris on March 15, 1975. His financial legacy was severely limited under Greek law, which dictated how much a non-Greek surviving spouse could inherit. After two years of legal wrangling, Kennedy eventually accepted a settlement of $26&#160;million from <a href="/wiki/Christina_Onassis" title="Christina Onassis">Christina Onassis</a>—Aristotle's daughter and sole heir— and waived all other claims to the Onassis estate.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Later_years_.281975.E2.80.931990s.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years_(1975–1990s)">Later years (1975–1990s)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Later years (1975–1990s)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>After the death of her second husband, Jacqueline returned permanently to the United States, splitting her time between Manhattan, <a href="/wiki/Martha%27s_Vineyard" title="Martha&#39;s Vineyard">Martha's Vineyard</a>, and the Kennedy compound in Hyannis Port, Massachusetts. In 1975, she became a consulting editor at <a href="/wiki/Viking_Press" title="Viking Press">Viking Press</a>, a position that she held for two years.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After almost a decade of avoiding participation in political events, she attended the <a href="/wiki/1976_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1976 Democratic National Convention">1976 Democratic National Convention</a> and stunned the assembled delegates when she appeared in the visitors' gallery.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> She resigned from Viking Press in 1977 following the false accusation by <i>The New York Times</i> that she held some responsibility for the company's publication of the <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Archer" title="Jeffrey Archer">Jeffrey Archer</a> novel <i><a href="/wiki/Shall_We_Tell_the_President%3F" title="Shall We Tell the President?">Shall We Tell the President?</a></i>, which was set in a fictional future presidency of Ted Kennedy and described an assassination plot against him.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Two years later, she appeared alongside her mother-in-law Rose Kennedy at <a href="/wiki/Faneuil_Hall" title="Faneuil Hall">Faneuil Hall</a> in Boston when Ted Kennedy announced that he was going to challenge incumbent president <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> for the Democratic nomination for president.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> She participated in the subsequent presidential campaign, which was unsuccessful.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following her resignation from Viking Press, Kennedy was hired by <a href="/wiki/Doubleday_(publisher)" title="Doubleday (publisher)">Doubleday</a>, where she worked as an associate editor under an old friend, <a href="/wiki/John_Turner_Sargent,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="John Turner Sargent, Sr.">John Turner Sargent, Sr.</a> Among the books she edited for the company are <a href="/wiki/Larry_Gonick" title="Larry Gonick">Larry Gonick</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cartoon_History_of_the_Universe" title="The Cartoon History of the Universe">The Cartoon History of the Universe</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> the English translation of the three volumes of <a href="/wiki/Naghib_Mahfuz" class="mw-redirect" title="Naghib Mahfuz">Naghib Mahfuz</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Cairo_Trilogy" title="Cairo Trilogy">Cairo Trilogy</a></i> (with Martha Levin),<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> and autobiographies of ballerina <a href="/wiki/Gelsey_Kirkland" title="Gelsey Kirkland">Gelsey Kirkland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-174">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> singer-songwriter <a href="/wiki/Carly_Simon" title="Carly Simon">Carly Simon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> and fashion icon <a href="/wiki/Diana_Vreeland" title="Diana Vreeland">Diana Vreeland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-174">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> She also encouraged <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_West" title="Dorothy West">Dorothy West</a>, her neighbor on Martha's Vineyard and the last surviving member of the <a href="/wiki/Harlem_Renaissance" title="Harlem Renaissance">Harlem Renaissance</a>, to complete the novel <i>The Wedding</i> (1995), a multi-generational story about race, class, wealth, and power in the U.S.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Reagans_with_Jackie_Kennedy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Reagans_with_Jackie_Kennedy.jpg/260px-Reagans_with_Jackie_Kennedy.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Reagans_with_Jackie_Kennedy.jpg/390px-Reagans_with_Jackie_Kennedy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Reagans_with_Jackie_Kennedy.jpg/520px-Reagans_with_Jackie_Kennedy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="647" data-file-height="444" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Reagans_with_Jackie_Kennedy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Onassis in 1985 with the President and First Lady, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Reagan" title="Nancy Reagan">Nancy Reagan</a></div></div></div>
<p>In addition to her work as an editor, Onassis participated in cultural and architectural preservation. In the 1970s, she led a historic preservation campaign to save <a href="/wiki/Grand_Central_Terminal" title="Grand Central Terminal">Grand Central Terminal</a> from demolition and renovate the structure in Manhattan.<sup id="cite_ref-Adler_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adler-129">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> A plaque inside the terminal acknowledges her prominent role in its preservation. In the 1980s, she was a major figure in protests against a planned skyscraper at <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Circle" title="Columbus Circle">Columbus Circle</a> that would have cast large shadows on Central Park;<sup id="cite_ref-Adler_129-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adler-129">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> the project was cancelled. A later project proceeded despite protests: a large twin-towered skyscraper, the <a href="/wiki/Time_Warner_Center" title="Time Warner Center">Time Warner Center</a>, was completed in 2003.
</p><p>Onassis remained the subject of considerable press attention,<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> most notoriously involving the paparazzi photographer <a href="/wiki/Ron_Galella" title="Ron Galella">Ron Galella</a>, who followed her around and photographed her as she went about her day-to-day activities; he took candid photos of her without her permission.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> She ultimately obtained a restraining order against him, and the situation brought attention to the problem of paparazzi photography.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> From 1980 until her death, Jacqueline maintained a close relationship with <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Tempelsman" title="Maurice Tempelsman">Maurice Tempelsman</a>, who was her companion and personal financial adviser; he was a Belgian-born industrialist and diamond merchant who was estranged from his wife.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 1990s, Onassis supported <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> and contributed money to his presidential campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-LH135_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LH135-181">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Following the election, she met with First Lady <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a> and advised her on raising a child in the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorker10132003_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorker10132003-182">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> In her memoir <i><a href="/wiki/Living_History_(book)" title="Living History (book)">Living History</a></i>, Clinton wrote that Onassis was "a source of inspiration and advice for me".<sup id="cite_ref-LH135_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LH135-181">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Democratic consultant <a href="/wiki/Ann_Lewis" title="Ann Lewis">Ann Lewis</a> observed that Onassis had reached out to the Clintons "in a way she has not always acted toward leading Democrats in the past".<sup id="cite_ref-SeattleTimes93_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SeattleTimes93-183">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Illness.2C_death_and_funeral"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Illness,_death_and_funeral">Illness, death and funeral</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Illness, death and funeral">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:OnassisGrave.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/OnassisGrave.jpg/220px-OnassisGrave.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/OnassisGrave.jpg/330px-OnassisGrave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/OnassisGrave.jpg/440px-OnassisGrave.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:OnassisGrave.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy Onassis's grave at <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="Arlington National Cemetery">Arlington National Cemetery</a></div></div></div>
<p>In November 1993, Jacqueline was thrown from her horse while participating in a <a href="/wiki/Fox_hunt" class="mw-redirect" title="Fox hunt">fox hunt</a> in <a href="/wiki/Middleburg,_Virginia" title="Middleburg, Virginia">Middleburg, Virginia</a>, and was taken to the hospital to be examined. A swollen <a href="/wiki/Lymph_node" title="Lymph node">lymph node</a> was discovered in her groin, which was initially diagnosed by the doctor to be caused by an infection.<sup id="cite_ref-death_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-death-184">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> The fall from the horse contributed to her deteriorating health over the next six months.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> In December, Onassis developed new symptoms, including a stomach ache and swollen lymph nodes in her neck, and was diagnosed with a type of cancer known as <a href="/wiki/Non-Hodgkin_lymphoma" title="Non-Hodgkin lymphoma">non-Hodgkin lymphoma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-death_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-death-184">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nytdeath_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytdeath-186">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> She began chemotherapy in January 1994 and publicly announced the diagnosis when she stated that the initial prognosis was good.<sup id="cite_ref-death_184-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-death-184">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> She continued to work at Doubleday, but by March the cancer had spread to her spinal cord and brain, and by May to her liver.<sup id="cite_ref-death_184-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-death-184">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nytdeath_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytdeath-186">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Onassis made her last trip home from <a href="/wiki/New_York_Hospital%E2%80%93Cornell_Medical_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Hospital–Cornell Medical Center">New York Hospital–Cornell Medical Center</a> on May 18, 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-death_184-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-death-184">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nytdeath_186-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytdeath-186">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> The following night at 10:15&#160;p.m., she died in her sleep in her Manhattan apartment at age 64.<sup id="cite_ref-nytdeath_186-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytdeath-186">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> In the morning, John F. Kennedy, Jr. announced his mother's death to the press, stating that she had been "surrounded by her friends and her family and her books, and the people and the things that she loved". He added that "She did it in her very own way, and on her own terms, and we all feel lucky for that."<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On May 23, 1994, her funeral Mass was held a few blocks away from her apartment at the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St._Ignatius_Loyola" class="mw-redirect" title="Church of St. Ignatius Loyola">Church of St. Ignatius Loyola</a>, the Catholic parish where she was baptized in 1929 and <a href="/wiki/Confirmation_in_the_Catholic_Church" title="Confirmation in the Catholic Church">confirmed</a> as a teenager.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> She was interred at Arlington National Cemetery in Arlington, Virginia, alongside President Kennedy, their son Patrick, and their stillborn daughter Arabella.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTobituary_14-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTobituary-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-death_184-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-death-184">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> President Bill Clinton delivered a eulogy at her graveside service.<sup id="cite_ref-nytburial_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytburial-190">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> At the time of her death, Onassis was survived by her children Caroline and John Jr., three grandchildren, sister Lee Radziwill, son-in-law <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Schlossberg" title="Edwin Schlossberg">Edwin Schlossberg</a>, and half-brother James Lee Auchincloss. She left an estate that its executors valued at $43.7&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popularity">Popularity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Popularity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Jacqueline Kennedy remains one of the most popular First Ladies. She was featured 27 times on the annual <a href="/wiki/Gallup_(company)" title="Gallup (company)">Gallup</a> list of the top 10 most admired people of the second half of the 20th century; this number is superseded by only <a href="/wiki/Billy_Graham" title="Billy Graham">Billy Graham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> and is higher than that of any U.S. President.<sup id="cite_ref-NationalGeographic_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NationalGeographic-192">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In 2011, she was ranked in fifth place in a list of the five most influential First Ladies of the twentieth century for her "profound effect on American society".<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, she ranked third place in a Siena College Institute survey,<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> behind <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt" title="Eleanor Roosevelt">Eleanor Roosevelt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abigail_Adams" title="Abigail Adams">Abigail Adams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, she was included in a list of the top ten influential U.S. First Ladies due to the admiration for her based around "her fashion sense and later after her husband's assassination, for her poise and dignity".<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy is seen as being customary in her role as First Lady,<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> though Magill argues her life was validation that "fame and celebrity" changed the way First Ladies are evaluated historically.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hamish_Bowles" title="Hamish Bowles">Hamish Bowles</a>, curator of the "Jacqueline Kennedy: The White House Years" exhibit at the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a>, attributed her popularity to a sense of unknown that was felt in her withdrawal from the public which he dubbed "immensely appealing".<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> After Kennedy's death, Kelly Barber referred to her as "the most intriguing woman in the world", furthering that her stature was also due to her affiliation with valuable causes.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Historian Carl Sferrazza Anthony summarized that the former First Lady "became an aspirational figure of that era, one whose privilege might not be easily reached by a majority of Americans but which others could strive to emulate".<sup id="cite_ref-NationalGeographic_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NationalGeographic-192">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Since the late 2000s, Kennedy's traditional persona has been invoked by commentators when referring to fashionable political spouses.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A wide variety of commentators have credited Kennedy with restoring the White House; the list includes <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Sidey" title="Hugh Sidey">Hugh Sidey</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-NationalGeographic_192-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NationalGeographic-192">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Leticia Baldridge,<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/Laura_Bush" title="Laura Bush">Laura Bush</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>  Kathleen P. Galop,<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> and Carl Anthony.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tina_Turner" title="Tina Turner">Tina Turner</a><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Joyner-Kersee" title="Jackie Joyner-Kersee">Jackie Joyner-Kersee</a><sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>  have cited Kennedy as influencing them.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Style_icon">Style icon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Style icon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jacqueline_Kennedy_after_State_Dinner,_22_May_1962.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Jacqueline_Kennedy_after_State_Dinner%2C_22_May_1962.jpg/220px-Jacqueline_Kennedy_after_State_Dinner%2C_22_May_1962.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Jacqueline_Kennedy_after_State_Dinner%2C_22_May_1962.jpg/330px-Jacqueline_Kennedy_after_State_Dinner%2C_22_May_1962.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Jacqueline_Kennedy_after_State_Dinner%2C_22_May_1962.jpg/440px-Jacqueline_Kennedy_after_State_Dinner%2C_22_May_1962.jpg 2x" data-file-width="647" data-file-height="845" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jacqueline_Kennedy_after_State_Dinner,_22_May_1962.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy at a <a href="/wiki/State_dinner" title="State dinner">State dinner</a> on May 22, 1962</div></div></div>
<p>Jacqueline Kennedy became a global fashion icon during her husband's presidency. After the 1960 election, she commissioned French-born American fashion designer and Kennedy family friend <a href="/wiki/Oleg_Cassini" title="Oleg Cassini">Oleg Cassini</a> to create an original wardrobe for her appearances as First Lady. From 1961 to 1963, Cassini dressed her in many of her most iconic ensembles, including her Inauguration Day fawn coat and Inaugural gala gown, as well as many outfits for her visits to Europe, India, and Pakistan. In 1961, Kennedy spent $45,446 more on fashion than the $100,000 annual salary her husband earned as president.<sup id="cite_ref-fashion_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fashion-212">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy preferred French couture, particularly the work of <a href="/wiki/Coco_Chanel" title="Coco Chanel">Chanel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crist%C3%B3bal_Balenciaga" title="Cristóbal Balenciaga">Balenciaga</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hubert_de_Givenchy" title="Hubert de Givenchy">Givenchy</a>, but was aware that in her role as First Lady, she would be expected to wear American designers' work.<sup id="cite_ref-bowles_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bowles-213">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>  After noting that her taste for Paris fashion was being criticized in the press, she wrote to the noted fashion editor <a href="/wiki/Diana_Vreeland" title="Diana Vreeland">Diana Vreeland</a> to ask for suitable American designers, particularly those who could reproduce the Paris look.<sup id="cite_ref-bowles_213-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bowles-213">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> After considering the letter, which expressed Kennedy's dislike of prints, and her preference for "terribly simple, covered-up clothes," Vreeland recommended <a href="/wiki/Norman_Norell" title="Norman Norell">Norman Norell</a>, who was considered America's First Designer, and was known for his high-end simplicity and fine quality work. She also suggested <a href="/wiki/Ben_Zuckerman" title="Ben Zuckerman">Ben Zuckerman</a>, another highly regarded tailor who regularly offered re-interpretations of Paris couture, and the <a href="/wiki/Sportswear_(fashion)" title="Sportswear (fashion)">sportswear</a> designer Stella Sloat, who occasionally offered Givenchy copies.<sup id="cite_ref-bowles_213-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bowles-213">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy's first choice for her Inauguration Day coat was originally a purple wool Zuckerman model that was based on a <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Cardin" title="Pierre Cardin">Pierre Cardin</a> design, but she instead settled on a fawn Cassini coat and wore the Zuckerman for a tour of the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> with Mamie Eisenhower.<sup id="cite_ref-bowles_213-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bowles-213">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In her role as First Lady, Kennedy preferred to wear clean-cut suits with a skirt hem down to middle of the knee, three-quarter sleeves on notch-collar jackets, sleeveless <a href="/wiki/A-line_(clothing)" title="A-line (clothing)">A-line</a> dresses, above-the-elbow gloves, low-heel pumps, and <a href="/wiki/Pillbox_hat" title="Pillbox hat">pillbox hats</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-fashion_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fashion-212">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> Dubbed the "Jackie" look, these clothing items rapidly became fashion trends in the Western world. More than any other First Lady, her style was copied by commercial manufacturers and a large segment of young women.<sup id="cite_ref-FirstLadies_38-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FirstLadies-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Her influential <a href="/wiki/Bouffant" title="Bouffant">bouffant</a> hairstyle, described as a "grown-up exaggeration of little girls' hair," was created by <a href="/wiki/Mr._Kenneth" title="Mr. Kenneth">Mr. Kenneth</a>, who worked for her from 1954 until 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-vf_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vf-214">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In her years after the White House, Kennedy underwent a style change; her new looks consisted of wide-leg pantsuits, large lapel jackets, <a href="/wiki/Bohemian_style" title="Bohemian style">gypsy skirts</a>, silk <a href="/wiki/Herm%C3%A8s" title="Hermès">Hermès</a> headscarves, and large, round, dark sunglasses. She often chose to wear brighter colors and patterns and even began wearing jeans in public.<sup id="cite_ref-LIFE_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LIFE-216">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> She set a new fashion trend with beltless, white <a href="/wiki/Jeans" title="Jeans">jeans</a> with a black <a href="/wiki/Turtleneck" class="mw-redirect" title="Turtleneck">turtleneck</a> that was never tucked in and instead pulled down over her hips.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Watching_the_America%27s_Cup_Race._Mrs._Kennedy,_President_Kennedy,_others._Off_Newport,_RI,_aboard_the_USS_Joseph_P...._-_NARA_-_194214.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Watching_the_America%27s_Cup_Race._Mrs._Kennedy%2C_President_Kennedy%2C_others._Off_Newport%2C_RI%2C_aboard_the_USS_Joseph_P...._-_NARA_-_194214.jpg/220px-Watching_the_America%27s_Cup_Race._Mrs._Kennedy%2C_President_Kennedy%2C_others._Off_Newport%2C_RI%2C_aboard_the_USS_Joseph_P...._-_NARA_-_194214.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Watching_the_America%27s_Cup_Race._Mrs._Kennedy%2C_President_Kennedy%2C_others._Off_Newport%2C_RI%2C_aboard_the_USS_Joseph_P...._-_NARA_-_194214.jpg/330px-Watching_the_America%27s_Cup_Race._Mrs._Kennedy%2C_President_Kennedy%2C_others._Off_Newport%2C_RI%2C_aboard_the_USS_Joseph_P...._-_NARA_-_194214.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Watching_the_America%27s_Cup_Race._Mrs._Kennedy%2C_President_Kennedy%2C_others._Off_Newport%2C_RI%2C_aboard_the_USS_Joseph_P...._-_NARA_-_194214.jpg/440px-Watching_the_America%27s_Cup_Race._Mrs._Kennedy%2C_President_Kennedy%2C_others._Off_Newport%2C_RI%2C_aboard_the_USS_Joseph_P...._-_NARA_-_194214.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2700" data-file-height="2700" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Watching_the_America%27s_Cup_Race._Mrs._Kennedy,_President_Kennedy,_others._Off_Newport,_RI,_aboard_the_USS_Joseph_P...._-_NARA_-_194214.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy and the President watching the <a href="/wiki/America%27s_Cup_Race" class="mw-redirect" title="America&#39;s Cup Race">America's Cup Race</a></div></div></div><p> Kennedy acquired a large collection of jewellery throughout her lifetime. Her triple-strand <a href="/wiki/Pearl#Pearls_in_jewelry" title="Pearl">pearl necklace</a>, designed by American jeweller <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Jay_Lane" title="Kenneth Jay Lane">Kenneth Jay Lane</a>, became her signature piece of jewelry during her time as First Lady in the White House. Often referred to as the "berry brooch," the two-fruit cluster brooch of strawberries made of rubies with stems and leaves of diamonds, designed by French jeweler <a href="/wiki/Jean_Schlumberger_(jewelry_designer)" title="Jean Schlumberger (jewelry designer)">Jean Schlumberger</a> for <a href="/wiki/Tiffany_%26_Co." title="Tiffany &amp; Co.">Tiffany &amp; Co.</a>, was personally selected and given to her by her husband several days prior to his inauguration in January 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> She wore Schlumberger's gold and enamel bracelets so frequently in the early and mid-1960s that the press called them "Jackie bracelets"; she also favored his white enamel and gold "banana" earrings. Kennedy wore jewelry designed by <a href="/wiki/Van_Cleef_%26_Arpels" title="Van Cleef &amp; Arpels">Van Cleef &amp; Arpels</a> throughout the 1950s,<sup id="cite_ref-jackiesjewelry.com_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jackiesjewelry.com-218">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> 1960s<sup id="cite_ref-jackiesjewelry.com_218-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jackiesjewelry.com-218">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> and 1970s; her sentimental favorite was the Van Cleef &amp; Arpels wedding ring given to her by President Kennedy.
</p><p>Kennedy was named to the <a href="/wiki/International_Best_Dressed_List" class="mw-redirect" title="International Best Dressed List">International Best Dressed List</a> Hall of Fame in 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Many of her signature clothes are preserved at the John F. Kennedy Library and Museum; pieces from the collection were exhibited at the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a> in New York in 2001. Titled  "Jacqueline Kennedy: The White House Years," the exhibition focused on her time as a First Lady.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2012, <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine included Kennedy on its All-TIME 100 Fashion Icons list.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i> included her on the list <i>10 Fashion Icons and the Trends They Made Famous</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honors_and_memorials">Honors and memorials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Honors and memorials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 210px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><caption>External video</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:ST49816218NOV1962.jpg" class="image"><img alt="ST49816218NOV1962.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/ST49816218NOV1962.jpg/210px-ST49816218NOV1962.jpg" decoding="async" width="210" height="272" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/ST49816218NOV1962.jpg/315px-ST49816218NOV1962.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/ST49816218NOV1962.jpg/420px-ST49816218NOV1962.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="983" /></a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://firstladies.c-span.org/FirstLady/37/Jacqueline-Kennedy.aspx">Jacqueline Kennedy</a>, First Ladies, Influence and Image, <a href="/wiki/C-SPAN" title="C-SPAN">C-SPAN</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul><li>A high school named <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis_High_School" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis High School">Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis High School for International Careers</a>, was dedicated by New York City in 1995, the first high school named in her honor. It is located at 120 West 46th Street between Sixth and Seventh Avenues, and was formerly the High School for the Performing Arts.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Public School 66 in the <a href="/wiki/Richmond_Hill,_Queens" title="Richmond Hill, Queens">Richmond Hill</a> neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Queens" title="Queens">Queens</a>, New York City was renamed in honor of the former First Lady.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The main reservoir in <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">Central Park</a>, located in Manhattan near her apartment, was renamed in her honor as the <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis_Reservoir" title="Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis Reservoir">Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis Reservoir</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Municipal_Art_Society" title="Municipal Art Society">Municipal Art Society</a> of New York presents the Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis Medal to an individual whose work and deeds have made an outstanding contribution to the city of New York. The medal was named in honor of the former MAS board member in 1994, for her tireless efforts to preserve and protect New York City's great architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> She made her last public appearance at the Municipal Art Society two months before her May 1994 death.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Bouvier_Kennedy_Onassis_Hall" title="Jacqueline Bouvier Kennedy Onassis Hall">Jacqueline Bouvier Kennedy Onassis Hall</a> at the <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_University" title="George Washington University">George Washington University</a> (her alma mater) in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_DC" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington, DC">Washington, DC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>'s East Garden was renamed the <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Kennedy_Garden" title="Jacqueline Kennedy Garden">Jacqueline Kennedy Garden</a> in her honor.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2007, her name and her first husband's were included on the list of people aboard the Japanese <i><a href="/wiki/SELENE" title="SELENE">Kaguya</a></i> mission to the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a> launched on September 14, as part of <a href="/wiki/The_Planetary_Society" title="The Planetary Society">The Planetary Society</a>'s "Wish Upon The Moon" campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> In addition, they are included on the list aboard <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter">Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter</a> mission.</li>
<li>A school and an award at the <a href="/wiki/American_Ballet_Theatre" title="American Ballet Theatre">American Ballet Theatre</a> have been named after her in honor of her childhood study of ballet.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The companion book for a series of interviews between mythologist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Campbell" title="Joseph Campbell">Joseph Campbell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bill_Moyers" title="Bill Moyers">Bill Moyers</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Power_of_Myth" title="The Power of Myth">The Power of Myth</a></i>, was created under her direction prior to her death. The book's editor, <a href="/wiki/Betty_Sue_Flowers" title="Betty Sue Flowers">Betty Sue Flowers</a>, writes in the <i>Editor's Note</i> to <i>The Power of Myth</i>: "I am grateful ... to Jacqueline Lee Bouvier Kennedy Onassis, the <a href="/wiki/Doubleday_(publisher)" title="Doubleday (publisher)">Doubleday</a> editor, whose interest in the books of Joseph Campbell was the prime mover in the publication of this book." A year after her death in 1994, Moyers dedicated the companion book for his PBS series, <i>The Language of Life</i> as follows: "To Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis. As you sail on to Ithaka." <a href="/wiki/Constantine_P._Cavafy#Ithaca" title="Constantine P. Cavafy">Ithaka</a> was a reference to the <a href="/wiki/Constantine_P._Cavafy" title="Constantine P. Cavafy">C.P. Cavafy</a> poem<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (December 2017)">self-published source</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> that <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Tempelsman" title="Maurice Tempelsman">Maurice Tempelsman</a> read at her funeral.<sup id="cite_ref-McFaddentemp_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McFaddentemp-234">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (December 2017)">self-published source</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li>A white gazebo is dedicated to Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis on North Madison Street in <a href="/wiki/Middleburg,_Virginia" title="Middleburg, Virginia">Middleburg, Virginia</a>. The First Lady and President Kennedy frequented the small town of Middleburg and intended to retire in the nearby town of Atoka. She also hunted with the Middleburg Hunt numerous times.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Portrayals">Portrayals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Portrayals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Jacqueline_Kennedy_Onassis" title="Cultural depictions of Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis">Cultural depictions of Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jaclyn_Smith" title="Jaclyn Smith">Jaclyn Smith</a> portrays Kennedy in the 1981 television film <i>Jacqueline Bouvier Kennedy</i>, depicting Kennedy's life until the end of the JFK presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> The film's producer Louis Rudolph stated an interest in creating a "positive portrait of a woman who I thought had been very much maligned," comments that were interpreted by John J. O'Connor of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> as erasing any chances of critique toward Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> Though Smith received praise for her performance,<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> with Marilynn Preston calling her "convincing in an impossible role",<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> Tom Shales wrote "Jaclyn Smith couldn't act her way out of a Gucci bag".<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Blair_Brown" title="Blair Brown">Blair Brown</a> portrays Kennedy in the 1983 miniseries <i><a href="/wiki/Kennedy_(miniseries)" title="Kennedy (miniseries)">Kennedy</a></i>, set during the Kennedy presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> Brown used wigs and makeup to better resemble Kennedy and said through playing the role she gained a different view of the assassination: "I realized that this was a woman witnessing the public execution of her husband."<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> Jason Bailey praised her performance,<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> while Andrea Mullaney noted her resemblance to Kennedy and general shyness.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> Brown was nominated for a television BAFTA as <a href="/wiki/British_Academy_Television_Award_for_Best_Actress#1980s" title="British Academy Television Award for Best Actress">Best Actress</a> and a Golden Globe as <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Award_for_Best_Actress_%E2%80%93_Miniseries_or_Television_Film#1980s" title="Golden Globe Award for Best Actress – Miniseries or Television Film">Best Actress in a Miniseries or Television Film</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Marianna Bishop, <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Michelle_Gellar" title="Sarah Michelle Gellar">Sarah Michelle Gellar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Roma_Downey" title="Roma Downey">Roma Downey</a> portray Kennedy in the 1991 miniseries <i><a href="/wiki/A_Woman_Named_Jackie" title="A Woman Named Jackie">A Woman Named Jackie</a></i>, covering her entire life until the death of Aristotle Onassis.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> Of being contacted for the role, Downey reflected: "I thought I was a strange choice because I didn't think I looked anything like her and I was Irish."<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Half of Downey's wardrobe was designed by Shelley Komarov<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> and Downey stated that though she had long harbored "great respect and admiration" for Kennedy, she was unaware of the troubles in her childhood.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Reviewer Rick Kogan praised Downey with doing "a surprisingly fine job in the demanding title role",<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> while Howard Rosenberg lamented Downey's performance failing to "pierce this thick glaze of superficiality".<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Ability_(magazine)" title="Ability (magazine)">Ability</a></i> credited the role with raising Downey's profile.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> In 1992, the miniseries won the Emmy Award for Outstanding Miniseries.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Rhoda_Griffis" title="Rhoda Griffis">Rhoda Griffis</a> portrays Kennedy in the 1992 film <i><a href="/wiki/Love_Field_(film)" title="Love Field (film)">Love Field</a></i>, set shortly before and in the aftermath of JFK's assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> It was Griffis' feature film debut.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> Griffis said she had been told by her orthodontist of her resemblance to Kennedy and was cast as her upon walking into the auditions for the role.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sally_Taylor-Isherwood" title="Sally Taylor-Isherwood">Sally Taylor-Isherwood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emily_VanCamp" title="Emily VanCamp">Emily VanCamp</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Joanne_Whalley" title="Joanne Whalley">Joanne Whalley</a> portray Onassis in the 2000 television miniseries <i>Jackie Bouvier Kennedy Onassis</i>, covering chronologically her entire life.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> Whalley prepared for the role by listening to recordings of Kennedy's voice along with working with a dialect coach; by the end of production, she developed an attachment to Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup>  Laura Fries assessed Whalley as lacking Kennedy's charisma despite being "soulful and regal" in her own right<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> while Ron Wertheimer viewed Whalley as being passive in the role and lamented "the filmmakers render Jackie as <a href="/wiki/Forrest_Gump" title="Forrest Gump">Forrest Gump</a> in a pillbox hat, someone who keeps passing close to the center of things without really touching -- or being touched by -- very much."<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stephanie_Romanov" title="Stephanie Romanov">Stephanie Romanov</a> portrays Kennedy in the 2000 film <i><a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Days_(film)" title="Thirteen Days (film)">Thirteen Days</a></i>, taking place during the Cuban Missile Crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> Philip French of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> noted her small role and being out of "the loop" was accurate of women's roles in "the early Sixties".<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> Laura Clifford called Romanov "unconvincing" in the role.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jill_Hennessy" title="Jill Hennessy">Jill Hennessy</a> portrays Kennedy in the 2001 television film <i>Jackie, Ethel, Joan: The Women of Camelot</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> Hennessy prepared for the performance by watching hours of archival footage of Kennedy and cited one of the reasons for her favoring of the miniseries was its distinctiveness in not focusing "strictly on the men or only on Jackie".<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> Reviewers Anita Gates<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> and Terry Kelleher<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> believed Hennessy brought "elegance" to the role while Steve Oxman panned the performance: "Hennessy simply doesn't possess the right natural grace. But this pic has a habit of telling us more that it shows us, and the actress manages to communicate the most important elements of the story without ever making it especially convincing."<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Bisset" title="Jacqueline Bisset">Jacqueline Bisset</a> portrays Onassis in the 2003 film <i><a href="/wiki/America%27s_Prince:_The_John_F._Kennedy_Jr._Story" title="America&#39;s Prince: The John F. Kennedy Jr. Story">America's Prince: The John F. Kennedy Jr. Story</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> Bisset noted the glasses she used during the film were holdovers from a prior role in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Greek_Tycoon" title="The Greek Tycoon">The Greek Tycoon</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> Neil Genzlinger observed that Bisset "should have known better" in taking on the role<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> while Kristen Tauer wrote Bisset portraying Onassis as a mother was a "different central light than many proceeding films".<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jeanne_Tripplehorn" title="Jeanne Tripplehorn">Jeanne Tripplehorn</a> portrays Onassis in the 2009 film <i><a href="/wiki/Grey_Gardens_(2009_film)" title="Grey Gardens (2009 film)">Grey Gardens</a></i> for a single scene.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> Tripplehorn said questions she had about <a href="/wiki/Edith_Bouvier_Beale" title="Edith Bouvier Beale">Edith Bouvier Beale</a> that she thought would be answered by being a part of the film remained unsolved.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> Tripplehorn received diverse reactions to her performance<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup>  while Brian Lowry noted her resemblance to Onassis and small role.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Katie_Holmes" title="Katie Holmes">Katie Holmes</a> portrays Kennedy in the 2011 miniseries <i><a href="/wiki/The_Kennedys_(TV_miniseries)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Kennedys (TV miniseries)">The Kennedys</a></i>, set during the Kennedy presidency and its 2017 sequel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Kennedys:_After_Camelot" title="The Kennedys: After Camelot">The Kennedys: After Camelot</a></i>, focusing on her life after 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> Mary McNamara<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> and Hank Stuever<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> regarded Holmes' performance with neutrality in their reviews of <i>The Kennedys</i> while Hadley Freeman called her "bloodless" in the role.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> Holmes stated reprising the role was a "bigger challenge" for having to act through later periods of Kennedy's life.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> When asked of the concurrent <i>Jackie</i> film, Holmes said, "I think its really exciting. It's just is a testament to how amazing Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis was and how much she meant to our country."<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> Holmes also stated both should be watched due to covering different periods of Jacqueline's life.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup>  In <i>The Kennedys: After Camelot</i>, Holmes' performance was viewed favorably by Daniel Feinberg<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> and Allison Keane<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> while Kristi Turnquist panned her.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Minka_Kelly" title="Minka Kelly">Minka Kelly</a> portrays Kennedy in the 2013 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Butler" title="The Butler">The Butler</a></i>, giving the film's protagonist Cecil one of her husband's neckties after his assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> Kelly said she was intimidated and scared taking on the role.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> Kelly admitted to having difficulty with perfecting Kennedy's voice, going "to sleep listening to her", and having discomfort with the wool clothing associated with the role.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ginnifer_Goodwin" title="Ginnifer Goodwin">Ginnifer Goodwin</a> portrays her in the 2013 television film <i><a href="/wiki/Killing_Kennedy_(film)" title="Killing Kennedy (film)">Killing Kennedy</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> Goodwin used intimate photos to better portray Kennedy and was concerned "to do her justice and to play her as accurately as possible without ever doing an impression of her".<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> Costar <a href="/wiki/Rob_Lowe" title="Rob Lowe">Rob Lowe</a> said of seeing Goodwin in the pink Chanel suit, "It made it real. If I were under any illusions about what we were doing, seeing her in that iconic moment was, I would say, sobering."<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> Tom Carson wrote that Goodwin's "trademark vulnerability humanizes Jackie considerably"<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> while Bruce Miller called her a miscast<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup> and Robert Lloyd<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> and Brian Lowry<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> panned her performance.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kim_Allen_(actress)" title="Kim Allen (actress)">Kim Allen</a> portrays Kennedy in the 2016 film <i><a href="/wiki/LBJ_(film)" title="LBJ (film)">LBJ</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> Ray Bennett noted in his review of the film that Allen was in a non-speaking role.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Natalie_Portman" title="Natalie Portman">Natalie Portman</a> portrays Kennedy in the 2016 film <i><a href="/wiki/Jackie_(2016_film)" title="Jackie (2016 film)">Jackie</a></i>, set during the JFK presidency and the immediate aftermath of the assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-Portman_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Portman-306">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> Portman admitted being intimidated taking the role and doing research in preparation for filming.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> Nigel M. Smith wrote that by portraying Kennedy, Portman was "taking on arguably the biggest challenge of her career".<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> Manohla Dargis,<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> David Edelstein,<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>  and Peter Bradshaw<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> praised her performance. Portman was nominated for Best Actress by <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy Awards</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/AACTA_Awards" title="AACTA Awards">AACTA Awards</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alliance_of_Women_Film_Journalists" title="Alliance of Women Film Journalists">AWFJ</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Austin_Film_Critics_Association" title="Austin Film Critics Association">AFCA</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Boston_Society_of_Film_Critics" title="Boston Society of Film Critics">BSFC</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup>  and won the category by the <a href="/wiki/Online_Film_Critics_Society" title="Online Film Critics Society">Online Film Critics Society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup>
